The Textual Proof of Prophethood and Its Sufficiency
Sanusi explains the naqli proof of prophethood and its sufficiency without miracles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the naqli proof of prophethood and why the testimony of an established prophet is sufficient without requiring a miracle.

Core Idea
A prophet's testimony about another prophet is decisive proof without miracles.

Sanusi now turns to the textual (naqli) proof of prophethood, which relies on the statements of previous prophets and scriptures. He argues that this proof alone is sufficient because the testimony of a prophet whose prophethood is established carries decisive weight. In this passage, you will see how he grounds this claim in the preserved mentions of the Prophet Muhammad in earlier scriptures despite later alterations.

Guided Path
1
Read and identify the components
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Identify the three elements that constitute the naqli proof according to Sanusi.
Look for: divine statement, prophets' mention, and charge to follow.
2
Understand the argument for sufficiency
Notice that Sanusi asserts the sufficiency of this proof without a miracle. He gives a reason: the testimony of an established prophet is decisive. Ask yourself why he contrasts this with miracles.
This counters the view that miracles are the only valid proof.
3
Examine the claim about preservation
Sanusi adds that the text of Muhammad's prophethood has been preserved in the Torah, Gospel, and Psalms despite attempts to alter them. Consider how this supports the earlier point about tawatur (recurrent transmission).
The frequency of mention makes complete removal impossible.
4
Reflect on the misconception
The common misunderstanding is that a prophet's testimony about another prophet is insufficient without a miracle. Sanusi directly refutes this. How does his argument rely on the prior establishment of the testifying prophet's prophethood?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما النقلي: فهو نصُّهُ تعالى على نبوَّتِهِ في الكتب الماضية (٢)، وذكر الأنبياء له، وإيصاؤُهُم على اتَّبَاعِهِ، وهذا الدليل وحده كافٍ بدونِ المعجزة؛ فإنَّ شهادةَ مَنْ ثَبَتَتْ نبوَّتُهُ لأحد بالنبوة دليل قاطع على ثبوتِ نبوَّتِهِ وإنْ لم تظهر معجزة على يده. وقد تواترت عنِ الأحبار الأخبار عن كُتُبِهم وأنبيائهم بنبوته قبل مبعثِهِ مُعيَّنينَ اسْمَهُ وبلدَهُ وصفته. وأيضاً فلم يزل نص نبوَّتِهِ والحمد لله موجوداً في التوراة والإنجيل والزبورِ إلى الآنَ معَ مبالغتِهم في تبديلها (٣)، وذلك يدلُّ على الاعتناء بأمرِهِ فيها، وكثرة ترديد ذكره فيها على وجه لا يزيلُ جميعه التبديل.
As for the textual proof: it is His statement, exalted is He, about his prophethood in the past scriptures, and the prophets' mentioning of him, and their charge to follow him. This proof alone is sufficient without a miracle, for the testimony of one whose prophethood is established regarding someone's prophethood is a decisive proof of his prophethood even if no miracle appears at his hand. The reports from the rabbis from their scriptures and their prophets about his prophethood before his mission, specifying his name, his land, and his description, have been recurrently transmitted. Also, the text of his prophethood, praise be to God, has not ceased to exist in the Torah, the Gospel, and the Psalms until now, despite their efforts to alter them, and that indicates the care for his affair in them, and the frequency of repeating his mention in them in a manner that alteration does not remove entirely.
Source pages p. 599

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the textual (naqli) proof of prophethood. Sanusi states that it consists of divine texts in previous scriptures explicitly stating Muhammad's prophethood, the mention of him by previous prophets, and their charge to their communities to follow him. He asserts that this proof alone is sufficient even without a miracle, because the testimony of a prophet whose prophethood has been proven (through his own miracles) regarding another person's prophethood is a decisive proof. Furthermore, the reports from Jewish rabbis based on their scriptures and prophets about Muhammad's prophethood before his mission, specifying his name, land, and description, have been transmitted via tawatur (recurrent transmission). He then adds that the text of his prophethood, praise be to God, has remained present in the Torah, Gospel, and Psalms up to the present time, despite the alterations made to these scriptures, demonstrating the special care for his mention in them and the frequency of his mention in a manner that alteration cannot remove entirely.

Key Terms
النقلي
المعجزة
شهادة
تواترت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the testimony of a prophet about another prophet is insufficient without observing a miracle from the latter.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that the testimony of an established prophet is a decisive proof, even without a miracle from the one being testified about.

Checkpoint

A) Because the texts are from reliable books.
B) Because the testimony of an established prophet is a decisive proof.
C) Because the miracles of the previous prophets suffice.
D) Because the texts are preserved from alteration.

Key Takeaway
The naqli proof of prophethood, based on the testimony of previous prophets and scriptures, is decisive and sufficient without requiring a miracle.

The Torah's Prophecy of a Prophet from the Brothers of Israel
Sanusi quotes and interprets a Torah verse to identify Muhammad as the promised prophet.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi interprets the Torah verse to show it refers to Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him).

Core Idea
The Torah foretold a prophet from the Arabs, and only Muhammad fits that description.

In this passage, Sanusi presents a specific textual evidence from the Torah that Muslims believe prophesies the coming of Prophet Muhammad. He quotes the verse, explains the key phrase 'from their brothers,' and argues that it must refer to the Arabs, pointing to the Prophet. This lesson will walk through his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Torah quote
Read the Arabic quote from the Torah and its English translation. Notice the phrase 'from among their brothers.'
This verse is from Deuteronomy 18:15-22.
2
Analyze 'from their brothers'
Understand that 'brothers' means a related but distinct group, so the prophet is not an Israelite.
3
Consider the two possibilities
Sanusi identifies Arabs and Romans as the only candidates. Evaluate why Romans are eliminated.
Only Job was a prophet among the Romans, and he lived before Moses.
4
Conclude with Muhammad
The only remaining possibility is the Arabs, so the prophet foretold is Muhammad.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ تبشير التوراة ] وقدِ اطَّلعَ علماؤنا رضيَ الله عنهم على كثير مِنْ تلك النصوص فيما بأيدي اليهود والنصارى مِنَ الكتبِ الآنَ: فمنها: أنَّ في المصحفِ الخامسِ مِنَ التوراة التي بأيديهم إلى الآنَ: ( قال الله تعالى لموسى بن عمرانَ: إِنِّي أقيم لبني إسرائيلَ مِنْ إخوتهم نبيّاً مثلك نبيّاً مثلَكَ، أجعل كلامي على فيهِ، فَمَنْ عصاه انتقمتُ منه ). فقوله تعالى: ( مِنْ إخوتهم ) يدلُّ على أنَّ هذا النبيَّ ليسَ مِنْ بني إسرائيل، فلا محالةَ أنَّهم إمَّا العرب أو الرومُ؛ أمَّا الرومُ فلم يكن منهم نبي سوى أيوب عليهِ السلامُ، وكانَ قبل موسى بزمانٍ، فتعيَّنَ أَنَّ المراد بالإخوة العربُ، فالذي بشَّرَتْ بهِ التوراة إذا نبينا ومولانا محمد صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ. قال بعضُ علماءِ قُرْطُبَةَ: ناظرني يوماً أحد أحبار اليهود وأهل الذكاء منهم في هذا، فقال: هذا كلُّه صحيح لا أجدُ فيهِ اعتراضاً عليهِ، غيرَ أَنَّهُ قال تعالى: ( سأقيم لبني إسرائيل )، ولم يكن محمد ـ صلّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ - رسولاً إلا إلى العرب، فقلتُ له: ما على الأرض مَنْ يجهلُ أمر محمد صلى الله عليه وسلَّمَ، وأَنَّهُ قالَ: بُعِثْتُ إِلَى الْأَحْمَرِ وَالْأَسْوَدِ، وَالْحُرِّ وَالْعَبْدِ، وَالذَّكَرِ وَالْأُنْثَى.
[Glad tidings of the Torah] Our scholars, may God be pleased with them, have come across many of those texts in the books currently in the possession of Jews and Christians: among them: that in the fifth book of the Torah which is in their hands to this day: (God, exalted, said to Moses son of 'Imran: I will raise up for the Children of Israel from among their brothers a prophet like you, a prophet like you; I will put My words in his mouth, and whoever disobeys him I will take vengeance upon him). So His statement, exalted is He: (from among their brothers) indicates that this prophet is not from the Children of Israel; inevitably they are either the Arabs or the Romans. As for the Romans, there was no prophet among them except Ayyub (Job), peace be upon him, and he was before Moses' time. So it is determined that the brothers referred to are the Arabs. Thus the one of whom the Torah gave glad tidings is our Prophet and Master Muhammad, may God bless him and grant him peace. A scholar from Cordoba said: One day a Jewish scholar, one of the intelligent among them, debated with me about this and said: All this is correct; I find no objection to it, except that He said: (I will raise up for the Children of Israel), and Muhammad, may God bless him and grant him peace, was sent only to the Arabs. So I said to him: There is no one on earth who is ignorant of the matter of Muhammad, may God bless him and grant him peace, and that he said: 'I have been sent to the red and the black, the free and the slave, the male and the female.'
Source pages p. 600

Commentary
This passage provides a specific example of the textual proof: a prophecy from the Torah. Sanusi first states that Muslim scholars have discovered many such texts in the Jewish and Christian scriptures. He then quotes Deuteronomy 18:15-22: 'God said to Moses: I will raise up for the Children of Israel from among their brothers a prophet like you...'. Sanusi analyzes the phrase 'from among their brothers'—since Israelites are the primary group, 'brothers' must refer to another nation, either Arabs or Romans. He argues that the Romans had no prophet after Moses except Job, who lived before Moses, so the only possibility is the Arabs. Thus the prophet is Muhammad. Then Sanusi relates a debate with a Jewish scholar from Cordoba. The scholar accepts the interpretation but objects that the prophecy says 'for the Children of Israel', whereas Muhammad was sent to the Arabs. The Muslim scholar responds that Muhammad's own hadith states he was sent to all of humanity: 'the red and the black, the free and the slave, the male and the female', meaning the entire world. Thus the objection is refuted, and the prophecy applies to Muhammad.

Key Terms
التوراة
الإخوة
التبشير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Torah prophecy could refer to a prophet from the Israelites themselves.
Correction: The phrase 'from their brothers' explicitly excludes Israelites, indicating a prophet from a related nation.

Checkpoint
Why does Sanusi conclude that the 'brothers' in the Torah prophecy refer to the Arabs rather than the Romans?
A) Because the Romans never had a prophet after Moses.
B) Because the Romans were not related to the Israelites.
C) Because only Job was a prophet among the Romans and he lived before Moses.
D) Both A and C.

Key Takeaway
The Torah contains a prophecy about a prophet from the Arabs, identified by Sanusi as Muhammad.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If you were to meet a Jew or Christian who denies Muhammad, could you now explain this prophecy from their own book? Would you feel obligated to share this knowledge? What would stop you from doing so?
?
This prophecy has been preserved even in the current Torah. Does that not make you wonder why so many people still reject Muhammad? How does this affect your own responsibility to convey the truth to others?

A Debate with a Jewish Scholar on the Universality of Muhammad's Mission
Sanusi recounts a debate with a Jewish scholar who objects that Muhammad was sent only to Arabs, and the response citing a hadith on universality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection raised by a Jewish scholar regarding the scope of Muhammad's prophethood and how it was refuted using the Prophet's own statement.

Core Idea
Muhammad was sent to all humanity, not just the Arabs, despite the prophecy addressing the Children of Israel.

After presenting the Torah prophecy, Sanusi relates a debate he had with a Jewish scholar. The scholar accepted the interpretation but objected that the prophecy says 'for the Children of Israel,' yet Muhammad was sent only to Arabs. This lesson examines the Muslim response: the Prophet stated he was sent to all people, so the objection is invalid.

Guided Path
1
Read the debate dialogue
Read the Arabic and English of the dialogue. Note the Jewish scholar's acceptance and his specific objection.
2
Understand the Jewish scholar's objection
He argues that the prophecy says 'for the Children of Israel', but Muhammad's mission was only to Arabs.
This is a valid historical observation.
3
See the Muslim scholar's response
The Muslim scholar cites a hadith where Muhammad says he was sent to all people: 'the red and the black, the free and the slave, the male and the female.'
This shows Muhammad's prophethood is universal.
4
Recognize the implication
The objection is refuted because Muhammad was sent to the Children of Israel as well, fulfilling the prophecy.

[ تبشير التوراة ] وقدِ اطَّلعَ علماؤنا رضيَ الله عنهم على كثير مِنْ تلك النصوص فيما بأيدي اليهود والنصارى مِنَ الكتبِ الآنَ: فمنها: أنَّ في المصحفِ الخامسِ مِنَ التوراة التي بأيديهم إلى الآنَ: ( قال الله تعالى لموسى بن عمرانَ: إِنِّي أقيم لبني إسرائيلَ مِنْ إخوتهم نبيّاً مثلك نبيّاً مثلَكَ، أجعل كلامي على فيهِ، فَمَنْ عصاه انتقمتُ منه ). فقوله تعالى: ( مِنْ إخوتهم ) يدلُّ على أنَّ هذا النبيَّ ليسَ مِنْ بني إسرائيل، فلا محالةَ أنَّهم إمَّا العرب أو الرومُ؛ أمَّا الرومُ فلم يكن منهم نبي سوى أيوب عليهِ السلامُ، وكانَ قبل موسى بزمانٍ، فتعيَّنَ أَنَّ المراد بالإخوة العربُ، فالذي بشَّرَتْ بهِ التوراة إذا نبينا ومولانا محمد صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ. قال بعضُ علماءِ قُرْطُبَةَ: ناظرني يوماً أحد أحبار اليهود وأهل الذكاء منهم في هذا، فقال: هذا كلُّه صحيح لا أجدُ فيهِ اعتراضاً عليهِ، غيرَ أَنَّهُ قال تعالى: ( سأقيم لبني إسرائيل )، ولم يكن محمد ـ صلّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ - رسولاً إلا إلى العرب، فقلتُ له: ما على الأرض مَنْ يجهلُ أمر محمد صلى الله عليه وسلَّمَ، وأَنَّهُ قالَ: بُعِثْتُ إِلَى الْأَحْمَرِ وَالْأَسْوَدِ، وَالْحُرِّ وَالْعَبْدِ، وَالذَّكَرِ وَالْأُنْثَى.
[Glad tidings of the Torah] Our scholars, may God be pleased with them, have come across many of those texts in the books currently in the possession of Jews and Christians: among them: that in the fifth book of the Torah which is in their hands to this day: (God, exalted, said to Moses son of 'Imran: I will raise up for the Children of Israel from among their brothers a prophet like you, a prophet like you; I will put My words in his mouth, and whoever disobeys him I will take vengeance upon him). So His statement, exalted is He: (from among their brothers) indicates that this prophet is not from the Children of Israel; inevitably they are either the Arabs or the Romans. As for the Romans, there was no prophet among them except Ayyub (Job), peace be upon him, and he was before Moses' time. So it is determined that the brothers referred to are the Arabs. Thus the one of whom the Torah gave glad tidings is our Prophet and Master Muhammad, may God bless him and grant him peace. A scholar from Cordoba said: One day a Jewish scholar, one of the intelligent among them, debated with me about this and said: All this is correct; I find no objection to it, except that He said: (I will raise up for the Children of Israel), and Muhammad, may God bless him and grant him peace, was sent only to the Arabs. So I said to him: There is no one on earth who is ignorant of the matter of Muhammad, may God bless him and grant him peace, and that he said: 'I have been sent to the red and the black, the free and the slave, the male and the female.'
Source pages p. 600

Commentary
This passage provides a specific example of the textual proof: a prophecy from the Torah. Sanusi first states that Muslim scholars have discovered many such texts in the Jewish and Christian scriptures. He then quotes Deuteronomy 18:15-22: 'God said to Moses: I will raise up for the Children of Israel from among their brothers a prophet like you...'. Sanusi analyzes the phrase 'from among their brothers'—since Israelites are the primary group, 'brothers' must refer to another nation, either Arabs or Romans. He argues that the Romans had no prophet after Moses except Job, who lived before Moses, so the only possibility is the Arabs. Thus the prophet is Muhammad. Then Sanusi relates a debate with a Jewish scholar from Cordoba. The scholar accepts the interpretation but objects that the prophecy says 'for the Children of Israel', whereas Muhammad was sent to the Arabs. The Muslim scholar responds that Muhammad's own hadith states he was sent to all of humanity: 'the red and the black, the free and the slave, the male and the female', meaning the entire world. Thus the objection is refuted, and the prophecy applies to Muhammad.

مناظرة
حبر
يهودي
أرسل إلى الأحمر والأسود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Muhammad's prophethood was limited to the Arabs.
Correction: The Prophet said he was sent to all people, including non-Arabs and all nations.

Checkpoint
How did the Muslim scholar respond to the objection that Muhammad was only sent to the Arabs?
A) He said the Torah prophecy was mistaken.
B) He cited a hadith showing Muhammad was sent to all people.
C) He argued the prophecy was allegorical.
D) He admitted the objection was valid.

Key Takeaway
The debate shows that prophethood can be universal even if the prophecy mentions a specific people.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that the Prophet was sent to all people. Is there any person or group you find difficult to include in your da'wah or brotherhood? What would the Prophet say to you about this?
?
The Jewish scholar accepted many truths but held back on one point. What is one belief or practice in Islam that you accept intellectually but have not fully submitted to? What is preventing you?
?
If you were the Jewish scholar hearing the hadith, would you have immediately accepted it or sought further evidence? How does your answer reflect your own willingness to submit to divine revelation even when it challenges your assumptions?

Proof from Torah for Muhammad's Prophethood
Sanusi presents a Torah-based proof for Muhammad's prophethood and answers an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Muslim scholars used the Torah to prove the prophethood of Muhammad and how they answered a key objection about the scope of his mission.

Core Idea
The Torah itself predicts a prophet from the Arabs, and his mission is universal.

This passage from Sanusi's work presents a fascinating interfaith argument. Muslim scholars examined the Torah and found clear references to a prophet like Moses who would come from the 'brethren' of Israel. Sanusi not only presents this proof but also recounts a debate where a Jewish rabbi objects, and the Muslim scholar refutes him using sound reasoning and the Prophet's own words.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the full translation. Let the argument's flow come through before interpreting details.
Pay attention to the phrase 'from their brethren' as it is the crux.
2
Identify the core proof
The key is the Torah verse: 'I will raise up for the Children of Israel from among their brethren a prophet like you.' Notice how 'brethren' excludes Israelites themselves.
The author then reasons that the only possible candidates for 'brethren' are Arabs or Romans.
3
Understand the objection
The rabbi objects that the verse says 'for the Children of Israel,' implying Muhammad's mission was only to them, not universal.
This objection challenges the Muslim claim that Muhammad was sent to all mankind.
4
See the refutation
The scholar refutes by citing the Prophet's own statement 'I was sent to all creation' and noting that the verse contains no restriction words like 'only'.
The specification of Israel is to reassure them that a prophet will come to them, not to limit his mission.
5
Appreciate the additional evidence
The passage ends with another Torah verse: 'God came from Sinai, and shone from Sagin' (Seir), which traditionally refers to the revelations given to Moses, Jesus, and Muhammad.
This reinforces the authenticity of the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تبشير التوراة
وقد اطلع علماؤنا رضي الله عنهم على كثير من تلك النصوص فيما بأيدي اليهود والنصارى من الكتب الآن
فمنها : أن في المصحف الخامس من التوراة التي بأيديهم إلى الآن : ( قال الله تعالى لموسى بن عمران : إني أقيم لبني إسرائيل من إخوتهم نبياً مثلك ، أجعل كلامي على فيه ، فمن عصاه انتقمت منه )
فقوله تعالى : ( من إخوتهم ) يدل على أن هذا النبي ليس من بني إسرائيل ، فلا محالة أنهم إما العرب أو الروم ; أما الروم فلم يكن منهم نبي سوى أيوب عليه السلام ، وكان قبل موسى بزمان ، فتعين أن المراد بالإخوة العرب ، فالذي بشرت به التوراة إذا نبينا ومولانا محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم
قال بعض علماء قرطبة : ناظرني يوماً أحد أحبار اليهود وأهل الذكاء منهم في هذا ، فقال : هذا كله صحيح لا أجد فيه اعتراضاً عليه ، غير أنه قال تعالى : ( سأقيم لبني إسرائيل ) ، ولم يكن محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم رسولاً إلا إلى العرب ، فقلت له : ما على الأرض من يجهل أمر محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم ، وأنه قال : بعثت إلى الأحمر والأسود ، والحر والعبد ، والذكر والأنثى
وهذا كتابه ينطق أنه مبعوث إلى الخلق كافة
قلت : وليس في قوله تعالى : ( سأقيم لبني إسرائيل ) ما يقتضي انحصار بعثه لهم فقط ; إذ ليس فيه شيء من أدوات الحصر ، وإنما عينوا بالذكر لدفع ما يتوهمون أنه لا يبعث إليهم من ليس منهم
ثم قال هذا العالم القرطبي : فقال ذلك الحبر : ما يمكنني ولا غيري دفع ذلك ، وبذلك أخبرنا أسلافنا اليهود عنه أنه قال : بعثت إلى الخلق كافة ، إلا فرقة من فرق اليهود يقال لها العيسوية ، تقول بنبوته ومعجزاته ، وتنكر أنه بعث إلى غير العرب ، عليه ، ثم عطف على يهودي إلى جنبه وقال : نحن قد جرى نشؤنا على اليهودية ، وبالله ما أدري كيف يكون الخلاص من أمر هذا العربي !
وفي التوراة أيضاً : ( جاء الله من سيناء ، وأشرق من ساغين )
Glad tidings of the Torah.
And our scholars, may Allah be pleased with them, have examined many of those texts in the books now in the hands of Jews and Christians.
Among them: that in the fifth book of the Torah which is in their hands now: (Allah Almighty said to Moses son of Amram: 'I will raise up for the Children of Israel from among their brethren a prophet like you, I will put My words in his mouth, and whoever disobeys him, I will take vengeance on him.').
So His statement, 'from their brethren,' indicates that this prophet is not from the Children of Israel. So inevitably they are either Arabs or Romans. As for the Romans, there was no prophet from them except Job, peace be upon him, and he was before Moses in time. So it is established that by 'brethren' is meant the Arabs. Thus the one the Torah gave glad tidings of is our prophet and master Muhammad, peace and blessings be upon him.
A certain scholar from Cordoba said: One of the Jewish rabbis and the intelligent among them debated me on this. He said: All of this is correct, I find no objection to it, except that He Almighty said: 'I will raise up for the Children of Israel,' and Muhammad, peace and blessings be upon him, was only a messenger to the Arabs. So I said to him: There is no one on earth who is ignorant of the matter of Muhammad, peace and blessings be upon him, and that he said: 'I was sent to the red and the black, the free and the slave, the male and the female.'
And this book of his proclaims that he was sent to all creation.
I said: And there is nothing in His statement, 'I will raise up for the Children of Israel,' that necessitates confining his mission to them only; for it contains none of the particles of restriction. Rather, they were specified by mention to repel what they might imagine, that no one from outside them would be sent to them.
Then this Cordoban scholar said: That rabbi said: Neither I nor anyone else can refute that. And thus our Jewish forefathers informed us about him, that he said: 'I was sent to all creation,' except a sect of the Jewish sects called al-'Isawiyya, who affirm his prophethood and his miracles, but deny that he was sent to other than the Arabs, upon him. Then he turned to a Jew beside him and said: We have grown up in Judaism, and by Allah, I do not know how to escape from the matter of this Arab!
And in the Torah also: (God came from Sinai, and shone from Sagin.)
Source pages p. 600

Commentary
This passage presents a proof from the Torah for the prophethood of Muhammad (peace be upon him) and answers an objection. The author begins by stating that Muslim scholars have examined the scriptures in the hands of Jews and Christians. The specific proof is from the fifth book of the Torah (Deuteronomy), where Allah tells Moses that He will raise up a prophet for the Children of Israel 'from among their brethren.' The key point is that 'brethren' here does not mean Israelites themselves; it refers to a related but distinct group. The author argues that the only possible candidates are Arabs or Romans. Since the Romans had no prophet after Moses except Job (who lived before Moses), the only remaining possibility is the Arabs, thus identifying the promised prophet as Muhammad. 

The author then recounts a debate between a Cordoban scholar and a Jewish rabbi. The rabbi accepts the interpretation but objects that the verse says 'for the Children of Israel,' implying Muhammad's mission is limited to them. The scholar responds with two points: first, Muhammad explicitly stated he was sent to all mankind ('the red and black, free and slave, male and female'), and the Quran confirms his universal mission. Second, the phrase 'for the Children of Israel' does not contain any particle of restriction (like 'only'); it simply specifies the people to whom he would be sent first or primarily, but does not exclude others. The rabbi concedes, noting that only a small Jewish sect called al-'Isawiyya denies Muhammad's universal mission while accepting his prophethood. The anecdote concludes with the rabbi expressing his inability to find an escape from the truth of this Arab prophet. 

Finally, the author cites another Torah verse: 'God came from Sinai, and shone from Seir,' which is a well-known prophecy about the manifestation of God's guidance through Moses (Sinai), Jesus (Seir/Seir), and Muhammad (Paran). This further supports the claim. Overall, the passage demonstrates how scriptural evidence from earlier revelations is used in kalam to prove the prophethood of Muhammad and to refute objections based on apparent limitations.

Key Terms
التوراة
بني إسرائيل
إخوتهم
تبشير
العيسوية
سيناء
ساغين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that 'from their brethren' means from the children of Israel themselves.
Correction: The passage explains that 'brethren' refers to a related but separate group, specifically the Arabs, because the Romans had no prophet after Moses except Job who came before him.

Checkpoint
What is the main argument from the Torah presented in this passage to prove the prophethood of Muhammad?
A) The Torah mentions a prophet like Moses who will come from the Children of Israel.
B) The phrase 'from their brethren' indicates the prophet is not from Israel, and only Arabs fit that description.
C) The Torah says God will come from Sinai and shine from Seir, which refers to Muhammad.
D) The Torah was given to Moses, and Muhammad fulfilled its laws.

Key Takeaway
The Torah's prophecy about a prophet from the brethren of Israel points to Muhammad, and his universal mission is not contradicted by the specific mention of Israel.

Testimony of a Jewish Scholar on the Universality of Muhammad's Mission
Sanusi records a Jewish scholar's testimony that Muhammad was sent to all mankind except a sect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi uses the testimony of a Jewish scholar and a linguistic argument to establish that Muhammad was sent to all creation, not just a specific group.

Core Idea
Even a Jewish scholar admits the claim of universal prophethood, and the Qur'an does not restrict it.

In this passage, Sanusi strengthens the argument for the universality of Muhammad's mission by presenting both a logical linguistic analysis of a Qur'anic verse and the remarkable admission of a Jewish scholar. The lesson shows how external testimony can reinforce a central Islamic doctrine when combined with careful textual reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without over-analyzing, then read the English translation. Notice the flow: a claim, an objection, a refutation, and a story.
The passage contains both a scholarly analysis and a narrative.
2
Analyze the linguistic argument about the verse
Focus on the sentence starting with 'قلتُ'. Sanusi argues that the verse about the Children of Israel does not restrict the mission because it lacks 'tools of restriction' (أدوات الحصر). Identify what those tools are and why their absence matters.
Tools of restriction include words like 'إنما' or 'فقط'.
3
Examine the Jewish scholar's testimony
Read the rabbi's words: he admits that his Jewish ancestors confirmed that Muhammad claimed to be sent to all creation. Consider the weight of this admission from a non-Muslim authority.
The rabbi is quoted from a narration by al-Qurtubi.
4
Understand the significance of the 'Īsawiyya exception'
The rabbi mentions a small sect (al-ʿĪsawiyya) that believed in Muhammad's prophethood but denied his universal mission. Notice that by singling out this sect, the rabbi implies that the mainstream Jewish tradition actually confirms the universal claim.
The exception proves the rule.
5
Reflect on the rabbi's closing remark
The rabbi ends by expressing confusion and anxiety: 'How will deliverance from this Arab be?' Reflect on how this emotional reaction strengthens the argument—it shows the truth is undeniable even to those who reject it.
The rabbi remains in Judaism despite the evidence, highlighting a personal struggle.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا كتابه ينطقُ أَنَّهُ مبعوث إلى الخلْقِ كافة(۱)
قلتُ : وليس في قولِهِ تعالى : ( سأقيم لبني إسرائيل ) ما يقتضي انحصار بعثِهِ لهم فقط ؛ إذْ ليسَ فِيهِ شيءٌ مِنْ أدواتِ الحصرِ ، وإِنَّما عينوا بالذكر لدفع ما يتوهمون أنَّهُ لا يُبْعَثُ إليهم مَنْ ليس منهم

الخلاص مِنْ أمر هذا العربي !
And this book of his proclaims that he was sent to all creation (1). I said: There is nothing in His saying: 'I will establish for the Children of Israel' that implies restriction of his mission to them alone, for there is in it nothing of the tools of restriction, rather they were specified by mention to repel what they imagined—that none other than themselves would be sent to them. And then this scholar al-Qurtubi said: That rabbi said: 'Neither I nor anyone else can repel that, and thus our Jewish forefathers informed us about him, that he said: I was sent to all creation (2), except a sect of the Jewish sects called al-ʿĪsawiyya, which affirms his prophethood and his miracles, but denies that he was sent to other than Arabs, upon him.' Then he turned to a Jew beside him and said: 'They? We are not upon anything of his: we have grown up in Judaism, and by Allah, I do not know how deliverance from the matter of this Arab will be!'
Source pages p. 601

Commentary
This passage presents an argument for the universality of Muhammad's prophethood. It begins with a statement that the Qur'an itself declares Muhammad sent to all creation. Then the author ('I said') addresses a potential objection: the verse 'I will establish for the Children of Israel' might seem to limit his mission to them. He explains that this verse does not use any restrictive particle, so it does not imply exclusivity; rather, it specifies them to counter the Jewish claim that no prophet would be sent to them. The author then quotes al-Qurtubi, who recounts a story: a Jewish rabbi (ḥabr) admits that the testimony of their ancestors confirms that Muhammad claimed to be sent to all mankind, except for a small sect called al-ʿĪsawiyya (who believed in his prophethood but denied his universal mission to non-Arabs). The rabbi then complains that they (the Jews) cannot refute this, but they remain in Judaism, expressing confusion about how to escape the challenge of this Arab prophet. The passage thus uses an adversarial admission to strengthen the case for Muhammad's universal prophethood. The key nuance is that even a non-believing Jewish scholar concedes the claim of universality, and the only exception (al-ʿĪsawiyya) is a tiny deviant sect, which actually reinforces the norm.

Misconception & Correction

Correction: Sanusi clarifies that the verse does not contain any 'tools of restriction' (adawāt al-ḥaṣr), so it cannot imply exclusivity.

Checkpoint

B) Because the verse does not contain any restrictive particles.
C) Because the Children of Israel are the only ones mentioned.
D) Because the verse was abrogated.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi uses a Jewish scholar's admission to reinforce the universality of Muhammad's prophethood, showing that even opponents confirm his claim.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The rabbi knew the truth about the Prophet's universal message but clung to his inherited Judaism. Is there an area of your life where you hold onto a habit, culture, or comfort that contradicts the clear teachings of the Qur'an and Sunnah? What would it cost you to let it go, and are you willing to pay that price today?
?
If a Jewish scholar could admit that the Prophet was sent to all creation except one small sect, what does that say about your own conviction? Do you truly live as if the Prophet's message is for every person you meet, or do you subconsciously limit it to those who are 'like you' in culture, ethnicity, or class?
?
The rabbi ended his testimony in despair: 'I do not know how deliverance from the matter of this Arab will be!' He saw the truth but felt trapped. Have you ever felt trapped between what you know is true and what you are accustomed to? Where is your heart holding back from fully submitting to the Prophet's authority? What step can you take today to break free?

Torah Prophecies of the Three Prophets
Sanusi interprets the Torah verse about God's coming from Sinai, Seir, and Paran as references to Moses, Jesus, and Muhammad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi uses a Torah verse about Sinai, Seir, and Paran as prophecies confirming the prophethood of Moses, Jesus, and Muhammad.

Core Idea
The Torah itself foretells the coming of Muhammad through the symbolism of Paran.

In this passage, Sanusi draws on a biblical prophecy to argue that Muhammad's prophethood was foretold in previous scriptures. He interprets a verse from Deuteronomy as referring to the revelations given to Moses, Jesus, and Muhammad. This shows the continuity of divine messages and provides a scriptural proof for Islam from the Torah.

Guided Path
1
Read the Torah citation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the verse: 'God came from Sinai, shone from Seir, appeared from Paran.' This is the basis of Sanusi's interpretation.
The verse is from Deuteronomy 33:2.
2
Identify the three interpretations
Sanusi maps each location to a prophet and scripture: Sinai → Moses and the Torah, Seir → Jesus and the Gospel, Paran → Muhammad and the Furqan (Qur'an). Understand how he derives this.
He uses the phrase 'came', 'shone', 'appeared' to indicate different revelations.
3
Reflect on the proof
Consider why this is strong evidence: it shows the Torah itself predicted Muhammad's prophethood. Also note the extra proof from Hagar and Ishmael being settled in Paran (Mecca).
This confirms that Paran is Mecca, so the 'appearance' refers to Muhammad's mission.

وفي التوراة أيضاً : ( جاءَ اللهُ مِنْ سَيناءَ ، وأشرقَ مِنْ ساغينَ) (۳)
واستعلنَ مِنْ جبال فارانَ ، ومعَهُ جماعةٌ مِنْ جبال فاران ((۱)
موسى عليه
فمجيتُهُ تعالى مِنْ جبل سَيناءَ : عبارةٌ عن مجيء أمرِه وشرعه لموسى عليه السلام ، وإنزاله التوراةَ عليهِ فِيهِ ؛ إِذْ عليهِ كلَّمَ اللهُ السلام ، على حد قولِهِ تعالى في القرآن : ﴿ وَجَاءَ رَبُّكَ وَالْمَلَكُ صَفًّا صَفًّا ) [الفجر : ٢٢] ، وإشراقُهُ مِنْ جبلِ ساغينَ : عبارة عن إنزاله الإنجيل على عيسى عليه السلام ، وإظهار دينِهِ ؛ لأنَّ ساغينَ مِنْ جبال الروم ، واستعلانُهُ مِنْ جبال فاران : عبارةٌ عن إنزالِهِ الفرقان ، وبعثِهِ نبيَّنا ومولانا :
محمداً صلى الله عليه وسلَّمَ منها ؛ إذ لا خلاف أنَّ فاران هي مكَّة
وقد قال الله تعالى في التوراة : ( إِنَّ اللهَ أسكن هاجر وابنها إسماعيل فاران ) ، وانظر تعبيرَهُ في التوراة عن ظهورِ شرع نبينا صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ بالاستعلانِ المؤذنِ بكمال الظهور ، فهو نظير
قوله تعالى في القرآنِ : لِيُظْهِرَهُ عَلَى الدِّينِ كُلِّهِ ﴾ [التوبة : ٣٣]
•
وقال في التوراة أيضاً لهاجرَ أُم إسماعيل حينَ دَعَتْهُ : ( قد سمعتُ خشوعكِ في إسماعيل ، وستكون يدة فوق يد الجميع ((٢) ، ومعلوم
And in the Torah also: 'God came from Sinai, and shone from Sāghīn (3), and appeared from the mountains of Paran, and with Him a multitude from the mountains of Paran (1).' So His coming, may He be exalted, from Mount Sinai is an expression for the coming of His command and law to Moses, peace be upon him, and His sending down the Torah upon him therein, since God spoke to him there, analogous to His saying in the Qur'an: 'And your Lord comes, and the angels rank by rank' (Al-Fajr 22). And His shining from Mount Sāghīn is an expression for His sending down the Gospel upon Jesus, peace be upon him, and manifesting his religion, because Sāghīn is among the mountains of the Romans. And His appearing from the mountains of Paran is an expression for His sending down the Furqān and sending our Prophet and Master Muhammad, may God bless him and grant him peace, from there, since there is no disagreement that Paran is Mecca. And God said in the Torah: 'Indeed God settled Hagar and her son Ishmael in Paran.' And note His expression in the Torah about the emergence of our Prophet's law as 'appearance' indicating complete manifestation, analogous to His saying in the Qur'an: 'To make it prevail over all religion' (At-Tawbah 33). And He said in the Torah also to Hagar, the mother of Ishmael, when she called upon Him: 'I have heard your supplication regarding Ishmael, and his hand shall be above the hand of all' (2), and it is known.
Source pages p. 601

Commentary
This passage provides scriptural evidence from the Torah for the prophethood of Muhammad. It cites a verse from Deuteronomy 33:2 describing God's manifestation from three locations: Sinai, Seir (here Sāghīn), and Paran. The author interprets each: (1) God's coming from Sinai refers to His giving the Torah to Moses; (2) His shining from Seir refers to the revelation of the Gospel to Jesus, because that area is in the land of the Romans; (3) His appearing from Paran refers to the revelation of the Qur'an (al-Furqān) to Muhammad, since Paran is identified with Mecca (Makkah). The author notes that the Torah itself states that God settled Hagar and Ishmael in Paran, confirming the identification. He also highlights the phrase 'appeared' (istaʿlana) implying full and manifest revelation, which parallels the Qur'anic verse 'to make it prevail over all religion'. Finally, he cites another Torah promise to Hagar that Ishmael's hand would be above everyone's hand, alluding to the ascendancy of the Islamic ummah. The passage thus uses biblical prophecies to confirm Muhammad's prophethood and the universal triumph of his message.

فاران
مكة
الفرقان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think these Torah verses refer to literal geographical events or to the physical movement of God.
Correction: Sanusi explains that these are symbolic expressions for the revelation of divine scriptures to the three prophets.

Checkpoint

B) The giving of the Torah to Moses
C) The birth of Jesus
D) The conquest of Mecca

Key Takeaway
Sanusi shows that the Torah itself prophesied the coming of Muhammad through the symbolism of Paran.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Torah itself testifies to the coming of Prophet Muhammad. Yet, how often do you truly feel the weight of that divine witness in your daily life? Are you living as someone who is part of the final triumphant revelation? What one concrete step can you take today to honor that legacy?
?
Sanusi explains that God's 'coming' is metaphorical—yet it carries immense reality: His command and law. When you read the Qur'an, do you sense that God's command is 'coming' to you right now, demanding a response? What would change if you approached every verse as a direct address from your Lord?
?
The prophecy of Ishmael's hand being above all is a promise of Islamic ascendancy. But are you personally striving for that superiority in your character, knowledge, and deeds? What is holding you back from being a true representative of that promise in your environment?

Prophethood
intermediate

Torah Prophecies of Moses, Jesus, and Muhammad
Sanusi interprets the Torah verse about Sinai, Seir, and Paran as references to Moses, Jesus, and Muhammad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi interprets the Torah verse about Sinai, Seir, and Paran as prophecies of the three prophets and their scriptures.

Core Idea
The Torah itself foretells the coming of Muhammad in veiled allegories.

Sanusi turns to the Torah (Old Testament) to demonstrate that prophethood of Moses, Jesus, and Muhammad was prefigured. He interprets a well-known verse from Deuteronomy as an allegorical reference to the giving of the Torah, the Gospel, and the Qur'an. This lesson guides you through his reasoning and the scriptural basis he uses.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once without translating. Notice the three key place names: Sinai, Saghīr (Seir), and Paran. The passage is dense, so take your time.
Some words may have variant spellings due to OCR.
2
Identify the three prophecies
Identify how each location is linked to a prophet: Sinai to Moses, Saghīr to Jesus, Paran to Muhammad. Sanusi explains each as an expression of God sending His law or scripture.
Saghīr is identified as Seir in the original verse.
3
Understand the allegorical interpretation
Notice that Sanusi uses the Qur'anic verse 'And your Lord comes' to explain that 'coming from Sinai' is allegorical for the coming of God's command. He applies the same method to the other locations.
4
Examine the additional prophecy about Ishmael
Read the second part about Hagar and Ishmael. Sanusi argues that the promise 'his hand will be above all' was fulfilled in Muhammad, who brought prophethood back to the descendants of Ishmael.
This reinforces the point that the Torah prophesies Muhammad specifically.
5
Reflect on the purpose
Consider why Sanusi uses these proofs. He is showing that the scriptures recognized by Jews and Christians contain clear signs of Muhammad's prophethood, fulfilling the expectation of a prophet from the line of Ishmael.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واستعلنَ مِنْ جبال فارانَ ، ومعَهُ جماعةٌ مِنْ جبال فاران موسى عليه فمجيئُهُ تعالى مِنْ جبل سَيناءَ : عبارةٌ عن مجيء أمرِه وشرعه لموسى عليه السلام ، وإنزاله التوراةَ عليهِ فِيهِ ; إِذْ عليهِ كلَّمَ اللهُ السلام ، على حد قولِهِ تعالى في القرآن : ﴿ وَجَاءَ رَبُّكَ وَالْمَلَكُ صَفًّا صَفًّا ﴾ [الفجر : ٢٢] ، وإشراقُهُ مِنْ جبلِ ساغينَ : عبارة عن إنزاله الإنجيل على عيسى عليه السلام ، وإظهار دينِهِ ; لأنَّ ساغينَ مِنْ جبال الروم ، واستعلانُهُ مِنْ جبال فاران : عبارةٌ عن إنزالِهِ الفرقان ، وبعثِهِ نبيَّنا ومولانا : محمداً صلى الله عليه وسلَّمَ منها ; إذ لا خلاف أنَّ فاران هي مكَّة وقد قال الله تعالى في التوراة : ( إِنَّ اللهَ أسكن هاجر وابنها إسماعيل فاران ) ، وانظر تعبيرَهُ في التوراة عن ظهورِ شرع نبينا صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ بالاستعلانِ المؤذنِ بكمال الظهور ، فهو نظير قوله تعالى في القرآنِ : لِيُظْهِرَهُ عَلَى الدِّينِ كُلِّهِ ﴾ [التوبة : ٣٣] وقال في التوراة أيضاً لهاجرَ أُم إسماعيل حينَ دَعَتْهُ : ( قد سمعتُ خشوعكِ في إسماعيل ، وستكون يدة فوق يد الجميع ، ومعلوم أنَّ إسماعيلَ عليهِ السلامُ وولدَهُ لم تكن أيديهم إلا تحت يدِ إسحاق ; لأنَّ في ولد إسحاقَ كانَتِ النبوَّةُ ، فلمَّا بعثَ الله نبينا ومولانا محمداً صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ .. جعلَ يدَ بني إسماعيل فوق يد الجميع ، وردَّ النبوَّةَ فيهم ، وأغناهم وعظَّمهم ، وبارك عليهم جداً ، كما قالَ التوراة
And He appeared from the mountains of Paran, and with Him a multitude from the mountains of Paran. Moses, upon him peace, so His coming, exalted be He, from Mount Sinai is an expression of the coming of His command and His law to Moses, upon him peace, and His sending down the Torah upon him therein; for on it God spoke to him, peace be upon him, analogous to His saying, exalted be He, in the Qur'an: 'And your Lord comes, and the angels, rank upon rank' [Al-Fajr 89:22]. And His shining from Mount Saghīr is an expression of His sending down the Gospel upon Jesus, upon him peace, and the manifestation of his religion; because Saghīr is among the mountains of the Romans. And His appearing from the mountains of Paran is an expression of His sending down the Furqan (the Qur'an) and His sending our Prophet and Master Muhammad, may God bless him and grant him peace, from it; for there is no disagreement that Paran is Mecca. And God, exalted be He, said in the Torah: 'Indeed, God settled Hagar and her son Ishmael in Paran.' And consider His expression in the Torah about the appearance of the law of our Prophet, may God bless him and grant him peace, with the verb 'appeared' which indicates complete manifestation. So it is analogous to His saying in the Qur'an: 'That He may make it prevail over all religion' [At-Tawbah 9:33]. And He also said in the Torah to Hagar, the mother of Ishmael, when she called upon Him: 'I have heard your supplication regarding Ishmael, and his hand will be above the hand of all.' And it is known that Ishmael, upon him peace, and his offspring had their hands only under the hand of Isaac; because prophethood was in the offspring of Isaac. Then when God sent our Prophet and Master Muhammad, may God bless him and grant him peace, He placed the hand of the children of Ishmael above the hand of all, and returned prophethood to them, and enriched them, and magnified them, and greatly blessed them, as the Torah said.
Source pages p. 602

Commentary
This passage is a proof of prophethood from the Torah (the Old Testament). The author interprets Deuteronomy 33:2, which says God came from Sinai, dawned from Seir, and shone forth from Paran. The author explains each phrase allegorically: 'coming from Sinai' refers to God's command and law given to Moses on Mount Sinai, analogous to the Qur'anic verse 'And your Lord comes' (89:22). 'Shining from Seir' (here written as Saghīn, a variant) refers to the sending down of the Gospel on Jesus in the region of the Romans. 'Appearing from Paran' refers to the sending down of the Qur'an (the Furqan) and the sending of Muhammad from Mecca, since Paran is identified with Mecca. The author then cites a tradition from the Torah (Genesis 21:17-18) where God tells Hagar that her son Ishmael's hand will be above all. The author explains that initially, the descendants of Isaac (the Israelites) held prophethood, but when Muhammad was sent, the hand (power and prophethood) of Ishmael's descendants (the Arabs) became supreme. Thus the Torah contains clear prophecies of Muhammad's mission.

Misconception: The locations in Deuteronomy 33:2 refer to a physical movement of God from one place to another.
Correction: Sanusi explains that the verse is allegorical, referring to the giving of scriptures and the sending of prophets, not a physical journey.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi demonstrates that the Torah contains allegorical references to Muhammad's prophethood, fulfilling the expectation of a prophet from Ishmael's line.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Torah verse says God 'appeared from Paran'—not physically, but through His command. When you read the Qur'an, do you sense that God is speaking directly to you through it, or is it just a text? What would change in your life if you approached every verse as God's personal address to you at this moment?
?
Sanusi highlights that the prophethood of Ishmael's descendants only came to full manifestation with Muhammad. If you are from the Islamic ummah, you inherit this legacy of being chosen to carry the final message. Do you live with the weight of that responsibility? In what specific area of your life could you better reflect the honor of being part of this community?
?
The Torah prophecy about Ishmael's hand being above all was fulfilled in God's timing. But when you look at the state of Muslims today—politically weak, divided, spiritually lethargic—how does that challenge your faith in God's promises? Are you willing to see your own personal struggles as part of a larger prophetic promise, and take action to strengthen your own connection to that legacy?

The Psalms Prophecy of Prophet Muhammad
Sanusi cites two Psalms as proof of Muhammad's prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi uses citations from the Psalms (Zabur) to argue that the Prophet Muhammad was foretold in earlier scriptures.

Core Idea
The Psalms describe a universal king whose attributes match Muhammad perfectly.

Sanusi draws on the Psalms that were present in the hands of Jews and Christians to show that their own scriptures foretell the coming of Prophet Muhammad. He cites two specific passages: one describing a global ruler who brings justice and mercy, and another mentioning 'a crowned praised one' from Zion. By interpreting these as references to Muhammad, Sanusi reinforces the argument that the Prophet was predicted in earlier revelations.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Start by reading the full Arabic passage from the Psalms and its literal English translation. Get a sense of the language and phrasing before diving into the interpretation.
2
Examine the first prophecy
Focus on the detailed description (similar to Psalm 72): a king who rules from sea to sea, receives tribute, delivers the poor, and has eternal dominion. Identify each attribute and ask: Could this apply to anyone else?
Note that the passage explicitly states 'his dominion will endure to the end of time.'
3
Analyze the second prophecy
Look at the brief citation about 'a crown that is praised' from Zion. Understand the metaphor: 'crown' symbolizes leadership, and 'Mahmud' (the Praised One) is a name of Muhammad. Consider why this points specifically to him.
This is a direct linguistic argument: 'Mahmud' is derived from the same root as 'Muhammad'.
4
Reflect on the interpretation method
Sanusi interprets these psalms as prophecies of Muhammad. Think about how he uses scriptural continuity as proof of prophethood. Notice that he does not claim these are exact words from the original Psalms but existing copies.
This shows the importance of scriptural evidence in proving prophethood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الزَّبورِ الذي بأيديهمُ الآنَ : ذكرُ صفةِ نبينا محمد صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ ، وقال فيه : ( ويجوزُ مِنَ البحرِ إلى البحر ، ومِنْ مُنقطع الأنهار إلى منقطع الأنهار ، وأنه يخرُّ أهل الجزائر بين يديه على ركبهم ، ويُجلِسُ أَعَداءَهُ بالترابِ ، وتأتيه ملوك بالقرابين ، وتسجد له وتَدينُ لهُ الأمم بالطاعة والانقيادِ ; لأنَّهُ يُخلّص المضطر البائسَ مِنْ أقوى منه ، وينقذُ الضعيف الذي لا ناصرَ لهُ ، ويرأفُ بالضعفاء والمساكين ، وأنَّهُ يعطي من ذهب بلادِ سبا ، ويُصلَّى عليهِ في كل وقت ، ويدوم أمره إلى آخر الدهر ) وفي الزَّبورِ أيضاً : (إِنَّ اللهَ أظهرَ مِنْ صيهونَ إكليلاً محموداً ) ، فالإكليل : كناية عن الرئاسة، ومحمود هو نبينا صلى الله عليهِ وسلَّمَ .
And in the Psalms (Zabur) which are now in their hands is the description of our Prophet Muhammad, may God bless him and grant him peace. It says in it: 'He will pass from sea to sea, and from the end of the rivers to the end of the rivers; and the inhabitants of the islands will fall on their knees before him; he will seat his enemies in the dust; kings will come to him with offerings; they will prostrate to him and the nations will owe him obedience and submission; because he will deliver the distressed and wretched from those stronger than him, and rescue the weak who have no helper; he will have compassion on the weak and poor; and he will give from the gold of Sheba; prayer will be made for him at all times; his dominion will endure to the end of time.' And also in the Psalms: 'Indeed, God has displayed from Zion a crown that is praised (Mahmud).' So the crown is a metaphor for leadership, and Mahmud (the Praised One) is our Prophet, may God bless him and grant him peace.
Source pages p. 603

Commentary
This passage is a proof of prophethood from the Psalms (Zabur). The author cites two psalms that he interprets as referring to Prophet Muhammad. The first is a detailed description (akin to Psalm 72) of a king who rules from sea to sea, receives tribute, delivers the poor, and whose reign is eternal. The author asserts that this description matches Muhammad. The second is a brief citation mentioning God displaying 'a crown that is praised' from Zion. The author explains that 'crown' is a metaphor for leadership, and 'Mahmud' (the Praised One) is a name of Muhammad. Thus, the Psalms foretell his prophethood and authority.

Key Terms
الزبور
الإكليل
محمود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the Psalms describe a past king like David or Solomon, or are not predictive.
Correction: Sanusi argues that the universal and eternal scope ('to the end of time') and the specific name 'Mahmud' (the Praised One) point uniquely to Prophet Muhammad, not to any past earthly king whose reign was limited.

Checkpoint
How does the author interpret 'إكليلاً محموداً' (a praised crown) in the Psalms?
A) As a literal crown of gold
B) As a metaphor for leadership referring to Muhammad
C) As a reference to the Torah
D) As a description of Jesus

Key Takeaway
Sanusi uses the Psalms to show that Prophet Muhammad was foretold by name (Mahmud) and described with universal attributes that no other prophet possessed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Before this lesson, did you truly believe that earlier scriptures foretold Prophet Muhammad? How does this Psalm prophecy affect your certainty about his prophethood? Are you now willing to study other scriptural proofs with the same openness?
?
The Psalm describes a king who delivers the distressed and has compassion on the weak. Does your own character reflect this trait? In what specific situation today can you embody the mercy and justice that Muhammad was foretold to bring?
?
If the Psalms indeed mention Muhammad by name and description, what does this demand from you regarding your relationship with the previous prophets and their books? How will you adjust your respect for them in light of this continuity?

Prophecies from Psalms about Muhammad
Sanusi presents prophecies from the Psalms about Muhammad's coming and interprets them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi uses biblical prophecies from the Psalms to support the claim that Muhammad is the promised prophet.

Core Idea
The Psalms foretell Muhammad's global leadership and mission.

Sanusi turns to the Hebrew scriptures to show that Muhammad's coming was anticipated in the Psalms. He interprets specific passages as direct references to the Prophet's life and mission. This lesson examines those prophecies and their interpretations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation to get the overall meaning.
Notice how Sanusi first sets the historical context before quoting the Psalms.
2
Understand the historical shift
Sanusi explains that prophethood was in Isaac's line until Allah sent Muhammad, returning it to Ishmael's line. This sets the stage for the prophecies.
3
Analyse the first prophecy
The first prophecy describes a universal ruler who crosses seas, receives tribute, and delivers the poor. Compare this to Muhammad's global mission.
Think about how each detail applies to the Prophet's life.
4
Analyse the second prophecy
The second prophecy mentions a 'praiseworthy crown' from Zion. Sanusi interprets 'crown' as leadership and 'praiseworthy' (Mahmood) as Muhammad.
This is a direct allusion to the Prophet's name.
5
Reflect on confirmation
Consider how these prophecies together provide evidence that Muhammad is the promised prophet from previous scriptures.

That Ishmael, peace be upon him, and his sons were only under the hand of Isaac because prophecy was in the offspring of Isaac. Then when Allah sent our Prophet and Master Muhammad, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, He placed the hand of the sons of Ishmael above the hand of all, and returned prophecy to them, and enriched them and magnified them, and blessed them greatly, as the Torah said.
Announcement of the Psalms
In
And in the Psalms which are now in their hands: there is a description of our Prophet Muhammad, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, and it says therein: 'He shall cross from sea to sea, and from the ends of the rivers to the ends of the rivers, and the people of the islands shall fall on their knees before him, and he shall seat his enemies in the dust, and kings shall come with offerings, and prostrate to him, and the nations shall submit to him in obedience and compliance; because he will deliver the distressed poor from those stronger than him, and rescue the weak who have no helper, and be kind to the weak and poor, and he will give from the gold of Sheba, and prayer will be upon him at all times, and his affair will endure until the end of time.'
And in the Psalms also: 'Indeed Allah has displayed from Zion a praiseworthy crown.' So the crown is a metaphor for leadership, and 'Praiseworthy' (Mahmood) is our Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace.
Source pages p. 603

Commentary
This passage provides proof from previous scriptures that Muhammad is the promised prophet. It first establishes that prophethood was in Isaac's line until Allah sent Muhammad, returning it to Ishmael's line. Then it cites two Psalms prophecies. The first long one describes a universal ruler who crosses seas, receives tribute, and delivers the poor—clearly pointing to Muhammad's global mission and conquests. The second prophecy mentions a 'praiseworthy crown' from Zion; the author explains 'crown' as a metaphor for leadership and 'praiseworthy' (Mahmood) as the Prophet's name. Thus, both prophecies are interpreted to confirm Muhammad's prophethood and leadership.

التوراة
الزبور
إكليل
محمود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think biblical prophecies do not refer to Muhammad at all.
Correction: Sanusi shows that the specific details in the Psalms match Muhammad's life and mission, such as his global reach, care for the weak, and perpetual legacy.

Checkpoint

A) The Torah
B) The leadership of the Muslims
C) Prophet Muhammad
D) King David

Key Takeaway
The Psalms contain clear prophecies about Muhammad's universal rule and leadership, confirming his prophethood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Read the description from the Psalms: 'He will deliver the distressed poor... rescue the weak who have no helper.' How does this match what you know of Muhammad's character? In what specific area of your life do you need to embody this quality toward those weaker than you?
?
The passage says 'prayer will be upon him at all times, and his affair will endure until the end of time.' Do you truly live as if Muhammad's teachings are eternal and relevant to every moment of your day? What is one habit you will change to make his sunnah more constant in your life?
?
When you encounter people of other faiths who do not accept these prophecies, does your heart feel secure in the truth of Islam or anxious? After seeing this evidence from their own scripture, what will you say to yourself when doubts whisper that Muhammad was not foretold?

The Nation with Double-Edged Swords
Sanusi asks rhetorical questions to identify the community described in Psalms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi uses a psalm verse and three rhetorical questions to argue that the Muslim community fulfills the biblical prophecy about a nation with double-edged swords.

Core Idea
The Psalms prophesied a nation with double-edged swords — and that nation is the Muslims.

Sanusi presents another biblical prophecy, this time from the Psalms of David, describing a nation that glorifies Allah in all postures, uses double-edged swords, and takes vengeance on idolaters. Then he asks three pointed questions to force the reader to recognise that this description fits only the followers of Prophet Muhammad. Let's examine the passage and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the prophecy carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the vivid imagery: loud voices, double-edged swords, binding enemies.
Pay attention to the phrase 'swords with two edges' — it is a key identifier.
2
Identify the three rhetorical questions
Sanusi asks three questions. List them and think about their implied answers. They all point to one conclusion.
3
Reflect on the author's argument
Why does Sanusi choose these specific questions? What does each one rule out? How does the commentary show that only Muhammad's community fits?
This connects to the broader proof of prophethood through previous scriptures.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الزَّبورِ أيضاً : ( ليفرح إسرائيل بخالقِهِ وبنو صيهونَ ؛ مِنْ أجل أنَّ الله اصطفى لهم أُمَّةً ، وأعطاهم النصر ، وشدَّدَ الصالحين منهم بالكرامة ، يُسبّحونَ اللهَ على مضاجعهم ، ويُكبّرونَهُ بأصوات مرتفعة ، بأيديهم سيوفٌ ذواتُ شُفْرينِ ؛ لتنتقم مِنَ الأمم الذينَ لا يعبدونَهُ ، يُوثقون الأمم بالقيود وأشرافهم بالأغلال )
فانظُرُ : مَنْ هذهِ الأمَّةُ التي سيوفها ذواتُ شُفْرِينِ ينتقمُ اللَّهُ بِهَا مِنَ الأمم الذينَ لا يعبدونَهُ ؟! ومَن المبعوث بالسيفِ مِنَ الأنبياءِ ؟! ومَنِ الذينَ يُكبِّرُونَ الله تعالى قياماً وقعوداً وعلى جنوبهم وبأصوات مرتفعة في الأذان ؟!
And in the Psalms also: 'Let Israel rejoice in its Creator, and the sons of Zion, because Allah has chosen for them a nation, and given them victory, and granted dignity to the righteous among them; they will praise Allah on their beds, and magnify Him with loud voices; in their hands are swords with two edges, to take vengeance on the nations who do not worship Him; they bind the nations with chains and their nobles with fetters.' So look: who is this nation whose swords are double-edged, by which Allah takes vengeance on the nations who do not worship Him? And who is the prophet sent with the sword? And who are those who magnify Allah Almighty standing, sitting, on their sides, and with loud voices in the call to prayer?
Source pages p. 604

Commentary
This passage presents a second prophecy from Psalms and then poses three rhetorical questions to force the reader to identify the community being described. The prophecy mentions a nation that rejoices in its Creator, magnifies Him loudly (including on their beds—i.e. in all postures), and carries double-edged swords to exact vengeance on idolaters, binding them. This description matches the Muslim ummah: they perform the adhan (call to prayer) with loud voices, glorify Allah in all states, and engage in jihad with the sword. The rhetorical questions imply that only Muhammad was sent with the sword among the prophets, and only his community practices such takbir and jihad. Thus, the prophecy is a clear reference to Islam.

Misconception: Some think this prophecy refers to the Jewish people (Israel) as they carry swords.
Correction: Sanusi shows that only the Muslim community fits because only they were sent with the sword for jihad, and only they call the adhan loudly, and they magnify Allah in all postures. The 'double-edged swords' symbolize armed struggle against idolatry, which is a distinctive feature of Islam.

Checkpoint

A) Moses

Key Takeaway
The prophecy in Psalms about a nation with double-edged swords and loud glorification of Allah refers to the Muslim ummah and its leader, Prophet Muhammad.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly rejoice in Allah and magnify Him in all postures—standing, sitting, on your side—as the psalm describes, or do you only remember Him when comfortable? The swords in your hand are not literal; what 'swords' of conviction do you carry to stand against injustice in your own life? Will you let laziness prevent the loud adhan of your heart from reaching those around you?
?
The prophecy says this nation binds the nobles with fetters. In your own life, are you chained by worldly status and comfort, or are you actively loosening the fetters of oppression and falsehood as this community is called to do? Whom are you 'binding' with the truth you have?
?
You claim to be part of this victorious nation. But when you hear the adhan, does it stir your soul to action? When you lie on your bed, do you praise Allah with hope of His reward and fear of His punishment? If not, are you truly worthy of being counted among those whom the psalm describes? What will you change today to live up to that description?

Prophecy of David's Son and Prayer for the Establisher of the Sunnah
Sanusi uses two Psalms verses to prove Muhammad's prophethood and David's prayer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how David's prayer for the establisher of the Sunnah confirms Muhammad's prophethood and clarifies Jesus' humanity.

Core Idea
David prayed for Muhammad to teach that Jesus is human, not divine.

This lesson presents two prophecies from the Psalms that point to Muhammad's mission. The first speaks of a mighty leader; the second reveals David's reaction to the news that his descendant Jesus would be called 'son of God'. Distressed, David prays for the one who would restore the truth—the establisher of the Sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage contains two distinct 'also' statements.
2
Identify the two prophecies
The first prophecy describes a mighty warrior with sword and law. The second is a divine announcement to David about a son who will be called 'son to Me'. Identify both.
3
Understand David's prayer
David is alarmed that his son Jesus will be called 'son of God'. He prays for the 'establisher of the Sunnah' to be sent to teach people that Jesus is merely human.
The prayer shows that only Muhammad can correct this misunderstanding.
4
Connect to Theology
This passage proves Muhammad's prophethood through David's request and also affirms the true human nature of Jesus, countering the claim of divinity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الزَّبورِ أيضاً : ( تقلَّدْ أيُّها الجبَّارُ السيف ؛ فإنَّ ناموسَكَ وشرائعك مقرونة بيمينك ، وسهامَكَ مسنونةٌ ، والأمم يخرُونَ تحتَكَ ) . وفيه أيضاً يقولُ الله تعالى لداودَ عليه السلام : ( سيُولَدُ لكَ ولد أدعى له أباً ، ويُدعى لي ابناً ، فقال داود عليه السلام : اللهم ؛ ابعث جاعل السنَّة كي يُعْلِمَ الناسَ أَنَّهُ بشرٌ ) ، فولد داودَ الذي ادعِيَ ابنا الله تعالى هو عيسى عليه السلام ؛ لأنَّهُ مِنْ أَحفادِ داودَ عليهِ السلامُ .
فاعتبر كيف دعا داود عليه السلام الله تعالى حينَ أَفْزِعَهُ ما أَخبرَهُ بِهِ مِنْ شأن ولدِهِ عيسى عليهِ السلامُ . . أن يبعث الله تعالى جاعل السنَّةِ
And in the Psalms also: 'Gird on the sword, O mighty one; for your law and statutes are bound to your right hand, and your arrows are sharpened, and the nations fall under you.' And in it also Allah says to David, peace be upon him: 'A son will be born to you; I will call him a father, and he will be called a son to Me.' So David, peace be upon him, said: 'O Allah, send the establisher of the Sunnah so that he may teach people that he is a human.' So the son of David who was claimed to be a son of Allah is Jesus, peace be upon him, because he is from the descendants of David, peace be upon him. So consider how David, peace be upon him, prayed to Allah when what he was told about his son Jesus, peace be upon him, alarmed him... that Allah would send the establisher of the Sunnah.
Source pages p. 604

Commentary
This passage uses two more Psalms verses to prove Muhammad's prophethood and clarify Jesus' status. The first verse ('Gird on the sword...') is presented as a prophecy about a mighty leader. The second verse describes a divine announcement to David: 'A son will be born to you; I will call him a father, and he will be called a son to Me.' The author identifies this son as Jesus (descendant of David). When David heard that this son would be called 'son of God', he was alarmed and prayed to Allah to send 'the establisher of the Sunnah' (i.e., Muhammad) to teach people that Jesus is merely human. Thus, the prayer links Jesus' correct understanding to Muhammad's mission. The passage ends mid-sentence, but the point is clear: David's prayer was answered by the coming of Muhammad.

Correction: Jesus is a human descendant of David, and David himself prayed for someone to teach this truth.

Prophecies of the Prophet from the Zabur
Sanusi interprets Psalms prophecies as foretelling Muhammad and clarifying Jesus' humanity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi uses verses from the Zabur (Psalms) as prophecies foretelling Prophet Muhammad and clarifying that Jesus is a human servant of God, not divine.

Core Idea
The Zabur foretells Muhammad's mission and refutes the divinity of Jesus.

Sanusi presents three prophecies from the Zabur that he argues point to Prophet Muhammad and the Muslim ummah. The first describes a nation with double-edged swords who praise God loudly—identifying the Muslims and their practice of adhan. The second addresses a mighty one with a sword. The third is a prophecy about David's son (Jesus) and David's prayer for a future prophet to clarify Jesus' humanity. This passage thus defends core Islamic beliefs using earlier scriptures.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the rhetorical questions Sanusi asks after the first quote.
The passage has a distinct rhythm with three separate quotes.
2
Identify the three prophecies
Find the three distinct scriptural quotes from the Zabur. Label them: Prophecy of the nation with swords, Prophecy of the mighty one with the sword, Prophecy of David's son.
Each is introduced by 'And in the Zabur also:'
3
Analyze Sanusi's interpretation
For each prophecy, note how Sanusi connects it to Muhammad or the Muslims. Ask: what imagery links to Islamic practice (sword, loud praise, adhan)?
The double-edged sword and loud voices are key.
4
Focus on the David prophecy
Examine the third prophecy: David is told his son will be called a son to God, and he prays for a prophet to clarify that this son (Jesus) is human. Sanusi identifies that prophet as Muhammad.
This directly counters Christian claims of Jesus' divinity.
5
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider why Sanusi uses the Zabur. How does this strengthen the case for Muhammad's prophethood and Jesus' humanity?
It shows consistency of divine message across scriptures.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الزَّبورِ أيضاً : ( ليفرح إسرائيل بخالقِهِ وبنو صيهونَ ؛ مِنْ أجل أنَّ الله اصطفى لهم أُمَّةً ، وأعطاهم النصر ، وشدَّدَ الصالحين منهم بالكرامة ، يُسبّحونَ اللهَ على مضاجعهم ، ويُكبّرونَهُ بأصوات مرتفعة ، بأيديهم سيوفٌ ذواتُ شُفْرينِ ؛ لتنتقم مِنَ الأمم الذينَ لا يعبدونَهُ ، يُوثقون الأمم بالقيود وأشرافهم بالأغلال ) فانظُرُ : مَنْ هذهِ الأمَّةُ التي سيوفها ذواتُ شُفْرِينِ ينتقمُ اللَّهُ بِهَا مِنَ الأمم الذينَ لا يعبدونَهُ ؟! ومَن المبعوث بالسيفِ مِنَ الأنبياءِ ؟! ومَنِ الذينَ يُكبِّرُونَ الله تعالى قياماً وقعوداً وعلى جنوبهم وبأصوات مرتفعة في الأذان ؟! وفي الزَّبورِ أيضاً : ( تقلَّدْ أيُّها الجبَّارُ السيف ؛ فإنَّ ناموسَكَ وشرائعك مقرونة بيمينك ، وسهامَكَ مسنونةٌ ، والأمم يخرُونَ تحتَكَ ) . وفيه أيضاً يقولُ الله تعالى لداودَ عليه السلام : ( سيُولَدُ لكَ ولد أدعى له أباً ، ويُدعى لي ابناً ، فقال داود عليه السلام : اللهم ؛ ابعث جاعل السنَّة كي يُعْلِمَ الناسَ أَنَّهُ بشرٌ ) ، فولد داودَ الذي ادعِيَ ابنا الله تعالى هو عيسى عليه السلام ؛ لأنَّهُ مِنْ أَحفادِ داودَ عليهِ السلامُ . فاعتبر كيف دعا داود عليه السلام الله تعالى حينَ أَفْزِعَهُ ما أَخبرَهُ بِهِ مِنْ شأن ولدِهِ عيسى عليهِ السلامِ . . أن يبعث الله تعالى جاعل السنَّةِ وكاشفَ الغُمَّةِ نبيَّنا محمداً صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ لِيُعْلِمَ الناسَ أَنَّ عيسى عليه السلام بشر ، عبد الله تعالى ، وليسَ باله .
And in the Zabur also: 'Let Israel rejoice in its Creator and the sons of Zion; because God chose for them a nation, and gave them victory, and strengthened the righteous among them with honor. They praise God on their beds, and magnify Him with loud voices. In their hands are double-edged swords, to take vengeance on the nations who do not worship Him, binding the nations with chains and their nobles with fetters.' So consider: Who is this nation whose swords are double-edged, by which God takes vengeance on the nations who do not worship Him? And who among the prophets was sent with the sword? And who are those who magnify God standing and sitting and on their sides and with loud voices in the adhan? And in the Zabur also: 'Gird on the sword, O mighty one; for your law and ordinances are bound to your right hand, your arrows are sharpened, and nations fall under you.' And in it also God Almighty said to David, peace be upon him: 'A son will be born to you; I will call him a father, and he will be called a son to Me.' So David, peace be upon him, said: 'O God, send the establisher of the sunnah to inform the people that he is a human.' So the son of David who was claimed to be a son of God Almighty is Jesus, peace be upon him, because he is from the descendants of David, peace be upon him. So consider how David, peace be upon him, prayed to God Almighty when what he was told about the matter of his son Jesus, peace be upon him, alarmed him... that God Almighty would send the establisher of the sunnah and the dispeller of distress, our Prophet Muhammad, may God bless him and grant him peace, to inform the people that Jesus, peace be upon him, is a human, a servant of God Almighty, and not a god.
Source pages p. 604

Commentary
This passage presents a series of proof texts from the Zabur (Psalms) interpreted as prophecies foretelling the coming of Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him). The author first quotes a passage describing a nation that praises God loudly and carries double-edged swords to take vengeance on those who do not worship God. The author then asks a rhetorical question: which nation has double-edged swords used by God for vengeance, which prophet was sent with the sword, and which people magnify God standing, sitting, and on their sides with loud voices? The implied answer is the Muslim ummah and Prophet Muhammad, as the Muslims fought in God's cause and the adhan is called out loudly. A second prophecy addresses the psalm addressed to a mighty one with a sword and arrows. Then the author cites a prophecy about David's son who would be called a son to God, which David understood as a future claim about Jesus. David prayed for God to send someone to clarify that Jesus is a human. The author concludes that this is a prophecy of Muhammad, who came to teach that Jesus is a human servant, not a god. The passage thus uses biblical prophecies to defend the Islamic doctrine of Jesus' humanity and Muhammad's prophethood.

The Paraclete Prophecy in the Gospel
Sanusi interprets Gospel references to the Paraclete as prophecies of Muhammad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi interprets Gospel references to the Paraclete as prophecies of Prophet Muhammad.

Core Idea
The Gospel's Paraclete is not the Holy Spirit but a human prophet—Muhammad.

This lesson presents Sanusi's use of biblical prophecies to argue for Muhammad's prophethood. He quotes the Gospel of John to show that the promised Comforter (Paraclete) fits the description of Muhammad. These passages are treated as evidence that earlier scriptures foretold his coming.

Guided Path
1
Read the Gospel quotes

2

List the attributes of the Paraclete mentioned: he speaks from hearing, rebukes the world, glorifies Jesus, brings interpretation, etc.
3
Connect to Muhammad
Consider how these attributes match the Islamic understanding of Prophet Muhammad: he received revelation, foretold future events, and brought the interpretation of scriptures.
This is the core argument for Muhammad's prophethood from biblical prophecy.
